        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.0c01652"><meta property="og:title" content="Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/acsami.0c01652.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries have been considered as one of ideal power sources for energy storage system. However, the choice of cathode material with good cycling stability and high capacity is limited. Herein, a nanocomposite of hierarchical mesoporous iron fluoride and reduced graphene oxide is prepared by an in situ approach. The as-prepared nanocomposite exhibits remarkably high discharge specific capacity of 227.5 mAh/g at 0.1C. Specifically, the discharge specific capacity of the sample still remains 87.5 mAh/g at a high rate of 15C after the 100th cycle. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements show that the addition of reduced graphene oxide can effectively reduce the charge transfer resistance and enhance the Na+ diffusion rate in the FeF3·0.33H2O nanoparticles. The structural changes of FeF3·0.33H2O is further investigated by ex-situ XRD, XPS, and ex situ high-resolution transmission electron microscopy."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/acsami.0c01652.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187ff4cb7c075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187ff4cb7c075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '9ab1b811-6934-4b7b-b5d0-eae79e05cbb9'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187ff4cb7c075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93187ff4cb7c075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride and Reduced Graphene Oxide Nanocomposite as Cathode Materials for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c01652", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">34</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries have been considered as one of ideal power sources for energy storage system. However, the choice of cathode material with good cycling stability and high capacity is limited. Herein, a nanocomposite of hierarchical mesoporous iron fluoride and reduced graphene oxide is prepared by an in situ approach. The as-prepared nanocomposite exhibits remarkably high discharge specific capacity of 227.5 mAh/g at 0.1<i>C</i>. Specifically, the discharge specific capacity of the sample still remains 87.5 mAh/g at a high rate of 15<i>C</i> after the 100th cycle. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements show that the addition of reduced graphene oxide can effectively reduce the charge transfer resistance and enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate in the FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanoparticles. The structural changes of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O is further investigated by ex-situ XRD, XPS, and ex situ high-resolution transmission electron microscopy.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D17538%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DChao%2BZhang%252C%2BShengli%2BAn%252C%2BWenting%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D15%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c01652%26title%3DHierarchical%2BMesoporous%2BIron%2BFluoride%2Band%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2BNanocomposite%2Bas%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D17546%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292533&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanocomposites"> Nanocomposites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20material">cathode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Iron+Fluoride&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_iron%20fluoride">iron fluoride</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Reduced+Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_reduced%20graphene%20oxide">reduced graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanocomposite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanocomposite">nanocomposite</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much attention in recent years because of the abundant resources of sodium on earth and have been regarded as one of the optimized power sources for energy storage systems (EESs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Although the electrochemical mechanism in SIBs is similar to that of LIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> the choice of cathode materials for SIBs are limited because of the larger ionic radius of sodium (0.97 Å) than that of lithium (0.68 Å). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> In past decades, cathode materials, such as sodium transition-metal oxide, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> polyanionic compounds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 8, 12 and 13">(8,12,13)</a> Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> and so on, have been studied in SIBs. However, some drawbacks, such as undesired stage phase transformation and oxygen generation during charge (e.g., for layered oxides), narrow ionic channels (e.g., for NaFePO<sub>4</sub>), and high hygroscopicity (e.g., for sulfates and PBs), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> prevent them from practical applications. Furthermore, the reversible specific capacities of these materials are too low to meet the application requirement. Iron-based fluorides have attracted much attention because of their high theoretical capacity of 712 mAh/g (with three-electron transfer) and high electromotive force of 2.7 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Particularly, FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O, one of the iron fluoride polymorphs with a specific hexagonal tungsten-bronze structure, shows excellent performance for the Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 17 to 20">(17−20)</a> However, the poor electronic conductivity of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O, due to the large band gap induced by the highly ionic character of the metal–halogen bond, causes its low capacity and the cyclic stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Two strategies have been developed to improve the electrochemical activity of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O. One strategy is adding conductive additives, such as graphene, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> CNT, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> etc. The other strategy is using nanosized active materials <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref27" aria-label="reference 5 and 27">(5,27)</a> to alleviate the volume effect and shorten the transport pathways of ions and electrons during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/desertion. Although a large number of nanomaterials, such as nanospheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> nanofibers, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> microspheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> and hollow yolk-like spheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> have been successfully prepared, they are still far from expectations. The iron-based fluorides with hierarchical structures have attracted attention because of their large space for electrolyte access and relieve the volume effect. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a> Recently, in situ synthesis of iron-based fluorides/conductive additives nanocomposites have drawn attention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> However, it is difficult to in situ synthesize nanocomposites, which consist of iron-based fluorides with hierarchical structure and conductive additives.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, the nanocomposites of hierarchical mesoporous FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanoparticles and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) sheets were fabricated by a reverse micelle method. Because the graphene oxide surface contains a large number of oxygen-containing sites (defects) that have a strong affinity with Fe<sup>3+</sup> cation, iron fluoride nanoparticles can grow in situ on graphene oxide sheets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Microscopic measurements clearly indicate good adhesion between the hierarchical mesoporous FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O and the rGO sheets. We use it as a cathode material of SIBs, which shows an initial discharge capacity of 227.5 mAh/g at 0.1<i>C</i>, the capacity of 87.5 mAh/g at a high current density of 15C after 100th cycle. A comprehensive explanation for the electrochemical mechanism of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O in SIBs is suggested by ex situ XRD, XPS, HRTEM, and SAED.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O@rGO nanocomposite and FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanoparticle were prepared by a reverse micelle method reported elsewhere. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref39" aria-label="reference 33 and 39">(33,39)</a> The iron acetylacetonate, Fe(acac)<sub>3</sub> (&gt;98%, Aladdin), and the hydrofluoric acid (40 wt %, Macklin) was respectively used as the iron source and the fluorine source. Fifty milligrams of graphene oxide was added to 10 mL of deionized water. With ultrasonication for 30 min, graphene oxide was sufficiently dispersed to obtain a brownish solution. Subsequently, the above solution was added to 100 mL of tetra ethylene glycol. The mixture was continuously stirred at 100 °C for 4 h to remove water and then cooled to 90 °C. Iron acetylacetonate (5 mmol) was added into the above mixture with stirring at 90 °C. Then, 3 mL of hydrofluoric acid was slowly dripped into the mixture. After continuously stirring at 90 °C for 16 h, the precursor was washed 4 times with ethyl alcohol. The obtained powder was then dried at 80 °C for 6 h. Finally, the precursor was calcined under a vacuum at 220 °C for 12 h to prepare the nanocomposite (denoted as FFH-G). The bare nanoparticle (denoted as FFH) was prepared by the same process without adding graphene oxide.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The XRD patterns of FFH-G and FFH were collected from 10 to 90° at a scan rate of 2° min<sup>–1</sup> by X-ray diffractor (XRD, Rigaku Smartlab, Cu Kα radiation, 40 kV, 150 mA). The thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, METTLER TOLEDO) was carried out under an argon atmosphere from 25 to 700 °C (10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>). The Raman spectroscopy was obtained by a LabRAM HR Evolution (HORIBA Jobin Yvon, 532 nm laser). The X-ray photoeletron spectroscopy (XPS, PHI Quantera) was performed to investigate the oxidation state of the samples and cycled electrodes. The morphology of the samples was observed by a scanning electron microscopy (SEM, ZEISS-Merling) and a transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Hitachi, HT-7700). The specific surface areas of samples were analyzed with the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method (Quantachrome, SI-MP). The high-resolution microscopy images and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) of as-prepared materials and cycled electrodes were acquired by a high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM, JEOL, JEM 2100F).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrodes were made by mixing active materials (FFH-G or FFH), carbon black (Super P), and polyvinylidene difluoride in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone with a weight ratio of 80:15:5. The resulting slurry was coated onto aluminum foil. The as-prepared electrode was then dried at 80 °C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. The CR2032-type coin cells were assembled with as-prepared electrode as cathode in Argon-filled glovebox. Sodium foil (Aladdin) and glass microfiber filters (Whatman) were used as the counter electrode and separators, respectively. NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution (1 M) in ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) with a volume ratio of 1:1 was used as the electrolyte. Cells were (dis)charged galvanostatically on a Neware battery test system with different rates in the potential range of 1.4—4.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and 1.0—4.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at room temperature. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed on a PARSTAT2273 electrochemical workstation (AMETEK) with a frequency ranging from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. The CV measurements were carried out with the same electrochemical workstation in the potential range of 1.4—4.0 and 1.0—4.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mVs<sup>–1</sup>. The three-electrode cells (ECC-REF model, EL-CELL) were assembled for CV measurements and EIS measurements. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test was taken on the Neware battery test system by discharging the cell for an interval of 10 min at 0.1<i>C</i> (1<i>C</i> = 200 mA/g), relaxing for 60 min, and repeating this process until the cutoff voltage 1.4 V.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis of nanocomposite is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The XRD patterns of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle is shown <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. All the diffraction peaks of the two samples could be indexed to FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O phase (JCPDS card No.76–1262, orthorhombic structure with <i>Cmcm</i> space group). Thermogravimetric analyses of nanocomposite and nanoparticle were conducted to calculate the content of reduced graphene oxide in the nanocomposite (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). From 25 to 700 °C, the weight loss of nanocomposite and bare nanoparticle are 24.77 and 19.85%, respectively. The reduced graphene oxide content can be calculated to be 6.14% of the total mass of nanocomposite (detailed calculation process is shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/suppl_file/am0c01652_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Scheme Illustration of the FFH-G preparation process; (b) XRD patterns and (c) thermogravimetric analyses of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Raman spectra of pure graphene oxide and FFH-G nanocomposite are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. The characteristic peaks at 1340 and 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> are respectively corresponding to the D band and G band. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value of FFH-G nanocomposite is 1.27, which is higher than that of graphene oxide (0.99), meaning that the interfacial bonds between FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O and reduced graphene oxide increases the disorder degree of reduced graphene oxide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref40" aria-label="reference 21 and 40">(21,40)</a> The XPS spectra of C 1s of graphene oxide and FFH-G nanocomposite are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, c. There are four types of carbon bonds in the C 1s spectra of graphene oxide and nanocomposite: (1) C–C/C═C at 284.8 eV; (2) C–O at 286.7 eV; (3) C═O at 287.2 eV; (4) COOH at 288.7 eV. The intensity of C–O, C═O, and COOH of nanocomposite is weaker than that of graphene oxide, suggesting that the graphene oxide is reduced during vacuum calcination.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Raman spectra of FFH-G nanocomposite and graphene oxide; (b, c) XPS spectra of C 1s of graphene oxide and FFH-G nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The microstructures of the as-prepared samples were characterized by SEM and TEM. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, c, the FFH nanoparticle exhibits a hierarchical mesoporous structure with uniform size of 150 nm. The mesoporous particles consist of interconnected nanosized primary particles, which has a large number of cracks or voids. The nanocomposite is composed of reduced graphene oxide sheets and hierarchical mesoporous FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanoparticles as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, d. The hierarchical mesoporous particle is uniformly fixed on the reduced graphene oxide sheets, since graphene oxide provides abundant nucleation sites for FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanocrystal. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms and the corresponding pore size distribution curves of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/suppl_file/am0c01652_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a, b</a>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/suppl_file/am0c01652_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>, the adsorption and desorption curves of two samples exhibit a typical IV-type curves, indicating the presence of mesoporous structure. The pore size of nanocomposite is around 5 nm (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f). The hierarchical mesoporous structure with some large cracks or voids is considered to be beneficial to accommodate the volume changes of the electrode and electrolyte infiltration. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b) SEM images of FFH nanoparticle and FFH-G nanocomposite; (c, d) TEM images of FFH nanoparticle and FFH-G nanocomposite; (e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of FFH-G nanocomposite; (f) BJH pore size distribution of FFH-G nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle were investigated by galvanotactic charge/discharge tests in a voltage of 1.4–4.0 V. The charge–discharge curves of nanocomposite at 0.1<i>C</i> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. The specific capacity calculation is based on the mass of nanocomposite. The initial discharge specific capacity of nanocomposite is 227.5 mAh/g. The cyclic voltammogram (CV) of FFH-G nanocomposite was measured in the potential range of 1.4–4.0 V. A cathodic peak appeared at 2.38 V in the first cathodic scan corresponds to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into active materials. During the reverse process, there is a anodic peak at ∼2.42 V in the anodic scan, which can be attributed to deintercalation process of Na<sup>+</sup>. Interestingly, a small anodic peak appears at ∼3.25 V in the anodic scan. Correspondingly, a small cathodic peak is appeared around 3.0 V during the second cathodic scan. The reactions corresponding to this pair of redox peaks is still not clear. In the subsequent cycles, the peaks of redox reaction are overlapped, indicating the good reversibility of electrode. The rate performance of as-prepared samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, . The discharge specific capacity of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle is 172.3 mAh/g and 97.2 mAh/g at 1<i>C</i>, respectively. The discharge capacity of FFH-G nanocomposite is 101 mAh/g after 100 cycles, which is 54.2% higher than that of bare FFH nanoparticles (65.5 mAh/g). The comparison of cycling performance of FFH-G nanocomposite with previous reports <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref21 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref21 ref43" aria-label="reference 2, 21 and 43">(2,21,43)</a> is shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, it indicates that the cycling performance of FFH-G nanocomposite is superior. Further increasing the current density to 15<i>C</i>, the discharge capacity of nanocomposite is stable, which shows 87.5 mAh/g after 100 cycles, but the nanoparticle is only 15 mAh/g. It indicates that the interfacial bonds between FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O and reduced graphene oxide not only significantly enhance the discharge capacity and cycle stability of material but also improve the rate capabilities.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1.
