class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Charge–discharge curves of the 1st and 2nd of FFH-G nanocomposite at 0.1 C; (b) cyclic voltammograms of FFH-G nanocomposite at a scan rate of 0.1 mV/s at 1.4—4.0 V; cycling performances of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle in 100 cycles at (c) 1<i>C</i> and (d) 15<i>C</i> at 1.4—4.0 V; (e) Nyquist plots of pristine FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle; (f) the equivalent circuit used for fitting the experimental EIS data.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements was carried out to explore interface behaviors of the two electrodes (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). The EIS pattern consists of one semicircle in the high-frequency region and a sloping line in the low-frequency region. The corresponding equivalent circuit for fitting is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f. <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> is ohmic resistance, which stands for the resistance of the electrolyte and electrode material. <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is the charge transfer resistance, corresponding to the semicircle in the high-frequency region. The Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) is related to the diffusion of sodium ion in the electrode. The double layer capacitance is represented by a constant phase element CPE. The ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>) of two electrodes is very close (6.274 and 6.327 Ω). But the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle is 208.3 and 542.5 Ω, respectively. These results suggest that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the nanocomposite is higher than nanoparticle sample, indicating that the addition of reduced graphene oxide can improve the electron conductivity of FFH-G nanocomposite. As addition of reduced graphene oxide, an effective conductive network is formed. The conductive network can ensure fast electron transport and limit the volume variation of the cathode material during cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p">The GITT analysis of as-prepared samples are performed to calculate the diffusion coefficient of Na ions (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) in the electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the discharge GITT curves of two samples. The <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows a single titration of GITT with schematic labeling of different parameters. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is calculated according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> derived by Weppner and Huggins as follows: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>Where <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is the diffusion coefficient of the mobile species. <i>M</i> and <i>m</i> are the relative molecular mass and the mass of the active material in the electrode, respectively. <i>V</i><sub>m</sub> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>) is the molar volume of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O. <i>A</i> (cm<sup>2</sup>) is the surface area of the active materials. <i>L</i> is the diffusion length. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, if the <i>E</i> (the potential V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) is linear with the <i>t</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> can be further simplified as <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at different discharge states can be calculated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. The <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of FFH-G nanocomposite is obviously higher than that of FFH nanoparticle in the whole discharge process. It suggests that the addition of reduced graphene oxide can improve the ionic conductivity of electrode, ultimately causing good electrochemical performance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) GITT curves of FFH-G nanocomposite and FFH nanoparticle; (b) <i>t</i> vs <i>E</i> profiles for a single GITT titration; (c) linear behavior of <i>E</i> vs <i>t</i><sup>–</sup><sup>1/2</sup>; (d) ion diffusion coefficients at different discharge states of two samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore the structural changes of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O at the discharge–charge processes, the ex situ XRD analysis of FFH-G electrode at different potentials was performed. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, two diffraction peaks marked with black dots is assigned to the aluminum current collector. The diffraction peaks of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O is shifted to lower 2θ angles after being discharged to 2.7 and 2.4 V, indicating the intercalation reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> into FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> At the same time, the intensity of characteristic peaks gradually decrease, which may be related to the stress and disorder causing by sodium ion insertion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> When further discharged to 1.4 V, the typical FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O diffraction peaks disappear, indicating that amorphized Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O formed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 49 and 50">(49,50)</a> Interestingly, the diffraction peaks of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O is reappeared when recharged to 4.0 V, indicating the reformation of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O, suggesting that the structural change of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O is highly reversible during charge/discharge. To further explore the redox reaction mechanism, the oxidation state of Fe element was estimated by XPS at different cycling states. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b, the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak of the as-prepared sample at 714.3 eV, correspond to the bonding energy of Fe(III)–F (713.9 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref51" aria-label="reference 36 and 51">(36,51)</a> When being discharged to 1.4 V, the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak shifts to lower bonding energy of 711.4 eV, implying the reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> to Fe<sup>2+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> After being recharged to 4.0 V, Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> can be detected. The XPS results of Fe 2p illustrates the reversible redox couples of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> participate charge compensation during the charge/discharge process.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) the ex situ XRD patterns of FFH-G electrode at different stages of the first cycle at 5 mA/g; (b) XPS spectra of Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for pristine and cycled FFH-G electrodes at 0.1<i>C</i>; HRTEM images of (c) pristine FFH-G electrodes, (f) after first discharge to 1.4 V, and (i) after recharge to 4.0 V; SAED patterns at corresponding cycling stages, (d) pristine FFH-G electrodes, (g) after first discharge to 1.4 V and (j) after recharge to 4.0 V; TEM images and corresponding respective elemental mappings of (e) pristine FFH-G electrodes, (h) after first discharge to 1.4 V, and (k) after recharge to 4.0 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The high-resolution transmission electron spectroscopy (HRTEM) was employed to further understand structural change of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O during the charge/discharge process. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, the particles are uniformly embedded on reduced graphene oxide sheets. The lattice fringes (0.321 nm) and the diffraction rings observed from SAED correspond well to those of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O phase, e.g., (220) planes. The element distribution of FFH-G nanocomposite was measured by energy disperse spectroscopy (EDS) as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e. It can be seen that Fe and F are uniformly distributed. When being discharged to 1.4 V, no obvious lattice fringe is observed, indicating the sample exhibits an amorphous structure (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f). At the same time, the SAED pattern consists of some diffuse diffraction rings, implying that FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O transforms to amorphous phase. This result is consistent with the ex-situ XRD (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The corresponding element distribution (as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>h) indicates Na<sup>+</sup> is intercalated into electrodes. After being recharged to 4.0 V, the lattice fringes and diffraction rings are reappeared and also corresponded well to those of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O phase. It indicates that the crystal structure of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O is absolutely restored, which is consistent with the result of the ex situ XRD. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>k, the sodium element may be caused by the residual electrolyte.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to further determine sodium storage mechanism of FFH-G nanocomposite, we performed the galvanostatic charge–discharge tests in the potential range of 1.0–4.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a, the initial discharge capacity of FFH-G nanocomposite is 530.2 mAh/g. Two voltage plateaus in the discharge curve corresponds to intercalation reaction and conversion reaction, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The cycle performance of sample at 0.1<i>C</i> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. The discharge capacity is quickly faded in the first 10 cycles. It indicates the electrode suffers from an irreversible structure degradation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> In the subsequent cycles, the discharge capacity decreases slowly. The discharge capacity of FFH-G nanocomposite is 90.1 mAh/g after 100th cycle and the capacity retention only 16.9%. The CV curves of FFH-G nanocomposite is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c. All the cathodic and anodic peaks in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c are consistent with the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a. There are two pairs of redox peaks appeared in the first cycle. The position of the first cathodic peak is same as that of 4.0–1.4 V (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), corresponding to the intercalation process of Na<sup>+</sup>. The second cathodic peak is at 1.05 V, which attributes to the conversion reaction. In the first anodic scan, two anodic peaks appear at 2.27 and 2.64 V. The cathodic peak at 1.05 V decreases in the subsequent cycles. Only one pair of redox peaks after 10 cycles remains, which can be attributed to the intercalation/extraction reaction, indicating that the conversion reaction has poor reversibility and the intercalation reaction is relatively reversible.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/medium/am0c01652_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Charge/discharge curves and (b) the cycle performance of FFH-G electrodes at 0.1<i>C</i> at 1.0–4.0 V; (c) cyclic voltammogram of FFH-G electrodes at the scan rate of 0.1 mV/s at 1.0–4.0 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/asset/images/large/am0c01652_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c01652&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, an in situ approach was used to prepare the nanocomposite of hierarchical mesoporous FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O and reduced graphene oxide. The FFH-G nanocomposite achieves an outstanding initial discharge capacity of 227.5 mAh/g at 0.1<i>C</i>. Especially, even at high current density of 15<i>C</i>, it still delivers an high discharge capacity of 87.5 mAh/g after the 100th cycle. The excellent electrochemical performance is attributed to enhance electronic conductivity and ion conductivity by the addition of reduced graphene oxide. The reversible Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O implies that FFH-G nanocomposite is a promising cathode material for SIBs with enhanced performance and stability.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c01652?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c01652</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed calculation process of TG, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms, and the BJH pore size distribution of FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O NPs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/suppl_file/am0c01652_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c01652/suppl_file/am0c01652_si_001.pdf">am0c01652_si_001.pdf (540.09 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c01652" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Here, we synthesized a low-cost, cobalt-free, Fe-substituted oxide material, Li(Li0.16Ni0.19Fe0.18Mn0.46)O2. XPS and DFT calcns. revealed that super iron FeIV exists in the delithiated state, and oxygen participates in the redox reaction in addn. to the Ni2+/Ni4+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ redox couples.  The anionic oxidn. preferentially occurred on oxygen with a Li-O-Li configuration and with oxidized Fe and Ni coordination.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2016.08.080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The FeF 3 ·0.33H 2 O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure as high-capacity cathode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure as high-capacity cathode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuangying; Wang, Xianyou; Jiang, Miaoling; Zhang, Rui; Shen, Yongqiang; Hu, Hai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The novel FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure is successfully prepd. using a 1-pot chem. method and mech. ball milling route.  The structure and morphol. of the samples are examd. by XRD, SEM, high-resoln. TEM (HRTEM) and selected-area electron diffraction (SAED).  The as-synthesized FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite exhibits a particle size of ∼1-5 μm, open mesoporous structure with av. pore diams. of 16.5 nm.  Although the particles size is large up-to micron scale, the sample displays excellent electrochem. performance due to its open mesoporous structure, which is beneficial to the Li+ diffusion and electron transportation.  When FeF3·0.33H2O/C is used as cathode material of LIBs, it exhibits a specific capacity of 276.4 mA-h g-1 and excellent cyclic performance with a capacity of 193.5 mA-h g-1 after 50 cycles at 1 C for LIBs.  Also, it also exhibits a high reversible capacity of 102 mA-h g-1after 40 cycles at 1 C for SIBs.  Therefore, FeF3·0.33H2O/C will be a promising cathode material for high-performance LIBs and SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Advanced Energy Materials.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Villaluenga, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hueso, K.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA00643K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, S.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An open-framework iron fluoride and reduced graphene oxide nanocomposite as a high-capacity cathode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ali, Ghulam; Oh, Si Hyoung; Kim, Se Young; Kim, Ji Young; Cho, Byung Won; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cathode materials with high capacity and good stability for rechargeable Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are few in no.  Here, a composite is reported of electrochem. active iron fluoride hydrate and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) as a promising cathode material for NIBs.  Phase-pure FeF3·0.5H2O is synthesized by a non-aq. pptn. method and a composite with rGO is prepd. to enhance the elec. cond.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultralong Layered NaCrO2 Nanowires: A Competitive Wide-Temperature-Operating Cathode for Extraordinary High-Rate Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Longwei; Sun, Xuan; Denis, Dienguila Kionga; Zhang, Jinyang; Hou, Linrui; Liu, Yang; Yuan, Changzhou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A cobalt and nickel co-modified layered P2-Na2/3Mn1/2Fe1/2O2 with excellent cycle stability for high-energy density sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chu, Shiyong; Chen, Yubo; Wang, Jie; Dai, Jie; Liao, Kaiming; Zhou, Wei; Shao, Zongping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">775</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">383-392</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering the abundant sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) demonstrate great potential in large-scale electrochem. energy storage sectors which capacity and cycle stability is highly dependent on their electrode materials.  Layered P2-type Mn-Fe-based oxide has been considered as one of the most promising cathodes for SIBs, while its unsatisfactory cycle performance and low energy d. strongly limit practical application.  Here, a Co/Ni modification strategy is proposed to optimize P2-Na2/3Mn1/2Fe1/2O2 (MF) from both aspects of reversible capacity and cycle stability, leading to the design of a new P2-Na2/3Mn1/2Fe1/4Co1/8Ni1/8O2 (MFCN).  In this new layered P2-type material, the introduction of Co effectively inhibits the irreversibility of the material, and the introduction of Ni relieves the Jahn-Teller effect and reduces Mn dissoln.  The simultaneous introduction of Co and Ni effectively improves the cycle stability of the electrode, indicated by the increase of the capacity retention rate from 51.5% for MF to 87.4% for MFCN over 100 discharge-charge cycles at the same c.d. of 130 mA g-1.  Meanwhile, the introduction of Ni effectively increases the discharge voltage with the middle discharge voltage increasing from 2.8 V (MF) to 3.3 V (MFCN), thereby improving the energy d. of the electrode.  All above features make the new material highly promising for use as a cathode material in practical SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2018.11.214"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The novel P3-type layered Na<sub>0.65</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>O<sub>2</sub> oxides doped by non-metallic elements for high performance sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The novel P3-type layered Na0.65Mn0.75Ni0.25O2 oxides doped by non-metallic elements for high performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yu; Wang, Xianyou; Li, Xiaolong; Yu, Ruizhi; Chen, Manfang; Tang, Ke; Zhang, Xiaohui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to the attractive advantage of safety and cost of sodium-ion batteries, lithium-ion batteries can be replaced by sodium-ion batteries with the ever-increasing demand of large-scale energy storage systems.  However, short cycle life and poor rate performance restrict sodium-ion batteries further com. application.  Cathode materials are one of the most important components of sodium-ion batteries, which have an unparalleled effect on the electrochem. performance of sodium-ion batteries.  Herein, the novel P3-Na0.65Mn0.75Ni0.25O2 cathode material doped by non-metal elements is synthesized via hydrothermal method and subsequent low temp. annealing process.  The as-prepd. P3-Na0.65Mn0.75Ni0.25O2 shows excellent electrochem. performance due to doping of non-metal elements.  It can be found that fluorine doped Na0.65Mn0.75Ni0.25F0.1O1.9 can distinctly suppress P3-O1 phase transition and deliver a higher reversible capacity of 163.7 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C, while boron doped Na0.65Mn0.75Ni0.25B0.1O2 transforms from P3 phase to a more stable P2 phase and exhibits remarkable cycle life and rate performance.  Therefore, this new strategy doping by means of non-metal elements provides a significant exploration for the development of the high capacity electrode materials of sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b19874"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Rate and Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries Based on Sponge-like Three-Dimensional Porous Na-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode Material</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tracking lithium transport and electrochemical reactions in nanoparticles</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang Feng; Yu Hui-Chia; Chen Min-Hua; Wu Lijun; Pereira Nathalie; Thornton Katsuyo; Van der Ven Anton; Zhu Yimei; Amatucci Glenn G; Graetz Jason</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1201</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Expectations for the next generation of lithium batteries include greater energy and power densities along with a substantial increase in both calendar and cycle life.  Developing new materials to meet these goals requires a better understanding of how electrodes function by tracking physical and chemical changes of active components in a working electrode.  Here we develop a new, simple in-situ electrochemical cell for the transmission electron microscope and use it to track lithium transport and conversion in FeF(2) nanoparticles by nanoscale imaging, diffraction and spectroscopy.  In this system, lithium conversion is initiated at the surface, sweeping rapidly across the FeF(2) particles, followed by a gradual phase transformation in the bulk, resulting in 1-3 nm iron crystallites mixed with amorphous LiF.  The real-time imaging reveals a surprisingly fast conversion process in individual particles (complete in a few minutes), with a morphological evolution resembling spinodal decomposition.  This work provides new insights into the inter- and intra-particle lithium transport and kinetics of lithium conversion reactions, and may help to pave the way to develop high-energy conversion electrodes for lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201002879"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of FeF3 Nanoflowers on CNT Branches and Their Application to High Power Lithium Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Gwon, Hyeokjo; Kim, Jongsoon; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5260-5264</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel nano-architecture of a CNT-FeF3 composite (FNCB) has been fabricated and demonstrated to be a promising cathode material for Li rechargeable batteries.  The CNT network was functionalized by formation of FeF3 nanoflowers on the CNT surfaces.  Electrochem. anal. of FNCB showed superior performance with the high sp. capacity of 210 mA-h/g at 20 mA/g and high power capability delivering about 150 mA-h/g even at 500 mA/g with voltage range of 2.0 to 4.5 V at room temp.  This is an unprecedentedly high rate capability for an FeF3-based electrode.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ generated FeF3 in homogeneous iron matrix toward high-performance cathode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, De-long; Wang, Heng-guo; Li, Yang; Xu, Dan; Yuan, Shuang; Huang, Xiao-lei; Zhang, Xin-bo; Zhang, Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A strategy for enabling highly insulated FeF3 as high performance cathode materials for NIBs is proposed and realized, through constructing metallic Fe and reduced graphene oxide (RGO) double enhancement conducting matrix, wherein both the metallic Fe and active FeF3 are in situ electrochem. generated from one FeF2 grain (as precursor of FeF3) to ensure their desired homogenous and intimate contact.  The efficacy of this concept is demonstrated by the superior electrochem. performance of the generated FeF3-Fe-RGO composite including high capacity of 150 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1, good cycle stability, and high power capability at room temp., which could be reasonably attributed to homogeneous conductive matrix composed of metallic Fe and RGO in the composite and esp. their ideal contact with the active FeF3 component.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A flowerlike mesoporous FeF3·0.33H2O with three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical nanostructure is successfully prepd. for the first time through a facile PEG-assisted solvothermal route.  By rationally regulating and controlling the amt. of PEG-400 in solvent, a series of nanostructured FeF3·0.33H2O materials with various morphologies and sizes is obtained. Furthermore, the electrochem. performances of the as-prepd. samples for Na-ion battery (NIB) are investigated and discussed. The excellent electrochem. performances can be attributed to the unique 3D hierarchical porous nanostructure and the small particle size, which can provide large electrode/electrolyte contact area and abundant Na storage sites, facilitate rapid electronic and ionic transportation as well as electrolyte penetration, and accommodate the vol. changes of the FeF3·0.33H2O cathode materials.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-templated formation of hollow yolk-like spheres iron fluoride as cathode material for high-performance Li-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Jinfang; Zhu, Licai; Chen, Shuyi; Li, Qiyang; He, Zhong; Cai, Zijian; Cao, Linshen; Yuan, Zhongzhi; Liu, Jincheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">FeF3 is one of the representatives of multi-electron cathode materials for Li-ion batteries, which has attracted extensive attention.  However, its poor rate performance and vol. adaptability during charge-discharge cycles hinder its practical application.  To solve these defects, hollow yolk-like spheres FeF3 with micro-nano architectures has been synthesized by a new self-templated solvothermal method.  Fe-glycerate precursor with hollow yolk-like spheres was 1st prepd. and subsequent performing ion exchange with NH4F as F source to form FeF3.  The formation mechanism of the hollow yolk-like spheres Fe-glycerate is attributed to Kirkendall effect.  The hollow yolk-like spheres FeF3 delivers a superior rate performance of 163 mAh/g at high c.d. of 712 mA/g, with a low capacity fading rate of 0.58% per cycle for 40 cycles.  It displays a high discharge capacity of 306 mAh/g at 71.2 mA/g.  The superior electrochem. performance is attributed to the large sp. surface area, short electrons and ions migration distance and hollow structure for electrolyte penetration deriving from the unique micro-nano hierarchical hollow yolk-like spheres structure.  This work provides a new and general method for controllable synthesis of hollow structure materials for high rate performance Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b11889"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Mesoporous Iron Fluoride with Superior Rate Performance for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Iron fluorides (FeFx) have attracted great interest in Li-ion batteries due to their high theor. capacity, low cost, and preferable cell safety.  However, their practical utilization is severely impeded by inferior electrode kinetics, leading to poor electrode cyclability and rate capabilities.  The major bottleneck should be lack of any effective engineering techniques to make reliable encapsulation and conducting matrix on sol. FeFx species.  Herein, we propose an applicable synthetic strategy where the massive prodn. of FeF3@carbon nanoreactors (total size: ∼60 nm) can be easily achieved by in situ engineering toward the core regions in hybrids, with the iron rust wastes and common solvents as raw materials.  Such functionalized configurations can well make up for the shortcomings of FeF3 species, enabling them with outstanding cathode behaviors involving excellent reversible capacity retention (∼270% higher than that of a bare FeF3 electrode after 600 cycles) and drastically enhanced rate performance.  This paradigm work provides a facile and scalable method to make superior and sustainable cathodes and, moreover, offers a feasible engineering protocol to make water-sol. species encapsulated into carbon matrix, not merely for batteries but also for other wide range of fields like catalysis, nanomedicine, etc.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1007/s42247-018-00021-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bensalah, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mustafa, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In situ generated MWCNT-FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.33H<sub>2</sub>O nanocomposites toward stable performance cathode material for lithium ion batteries</span>. <i>Emergent Materials.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">59</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">66</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Development of elec. vehicles and portable electronic devices during the past decade calls for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with enhanced energy d. and higher stability.  Integration of FeF3 phases and carbon structures leads to promising cathode materials for LIBs with high voltage, capacity, and power.  In this study, FeF3·0.33H2O nanoparticles were synthesized on reduced graphite oxide (rGO) nanosheets using an in situ approach.  By chem. tuning the interfacial bonding between FeF3·0.33H2O and rGO, high particle loading and enhanced cycling stability are successfully achieved.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a potential multi-electron electrode material for next generation lithium ion batteries, iron fluoride (FeF3) and its analogs are attracting much more attentions.  Their microstructures are the key to achieve good electrochem. performances.  In this work, FeF3·3H2O nano-flakes precursor with high crystallinity and flower-like morphol. is synthesized successfully, by a liq. pptn. method using Fe(NO3)3·9H2O and NH4HF2 as raw materials.  The formation and the crystal growth mechanisms of the FeF3·3H2O precursors are investigated and discussed.  After different temp. heat-treatment and followed by ball-milling with Super P, the as-prepd. FeF3.0·33H2O/C and FeF3/C nanocomposites are used as cathode materials for lithium ion batteries.  The FeF3.0·33H2O/C nanocomposite exhibits a noticeable initial specific capacity of 187.1 mAh g-1 and reversible specific capacity of 172.3 mAh g-1 at .1 C within a potential range of 2.0-4.5 V.  The capacity retention is as high as 97.33% after 50 cycles.  Combined with HRTEM test, it confirms that the hydration water is not harmful but useful, namely, the tunnel phase formed with the hydration water is crucial to unobstructed Li+ diffusion, and therefore leading to excellent electrochem. performances.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201506394"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Trogadas, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramani, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Strasser, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fuller, T.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hierarchical nanomaterials are highly suitable as electrocatalysts and electrocatalyst supports in electrochem. energy conversion devices.  The intrinsic kinetics of an electrocatalyst are assocd. with the nanostructure of the active phase and the support, while the overall properties are also affected by the meso-structure.  Therefore, both structures need to be controlled.  A comparative state-of-the-art review of catalysts and supports is provided along with detailed synthesis methods.  To further improve the design of these hierarchical nanomaterials, in-depth research on the effect of materials architecture on reaction and transport kinetics is necessary.  Inspiration can be derived from nature, which is full of very effective hierarchical structures.  Developing fundamental understanding of how desired properties of biol. systems are related to their hierarchical architecture can guide the development of novel catalytic nanomaterials and nature-inspired electrochem. devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/cm304127c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Top-Down Synthesis of Open Framework Fluoride for Lithium and Sodium Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Origins of Large Voltage Hysteresis in High-Energy-Density Metal Fluoride Lithium-Ion Battery Conversion Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Linsen; Jacobs, Ryan; Gao, Peng; Gan, Liyang; Wang, Feng; Morgan, Dane; Jin, Song</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal fluorides and oxides can store multiple lithium ions through conversion chem. to enable high-energy-d. lithium-ion batteries.  However, their practical applications have been hindered by an unusually large voltage hysteresis between charge and discharge voltage profiles and the consequent low-energy efficiency (&lt;80%).  The phys. origins of such hysteresis are rarely studied and poorly understood.  Here we employ in situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy, transmission electron microscopy, d. functional theory calcns., and galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique to first correlate the voltage profile of iron fluoride (FeF3), a representative conversion electrode material, with evolution and spatial distribution of intermediate phases in the electrode.  The results reveal that, contrary to conventional belief, the phase evolution in the electrode is sym. during discharge and charge.  However, the spatial evolution of the electrochem. active phases, which is controlled by reaction kinetics, is different.  We further propose that the voltage hysteresis in the FeF3 electrode is kinetic in nature.  It is the result of ohmic voltage drop, reaction overpotential, and different spatial distributions of electrochem. active phases (i.e., compositional inhomogeneity).  Therefore, the large hysteresis can be expected to be mitigated by rational design and optimization of material microstructure and electrode architecture to improve the energy efficiency of lithium-ion batteries based on conversion chem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.01.080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, B.                 10.1016/j.electacta.2016.01.080</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D191%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D307-316%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DG.%2BAli%26author%3DJ.-H.%2BLee%26author%3DB.%2BW.%2BCho%26author%3DK.-W.%2BNam%26author%3DD.%2BAhn%26author%3DW.%2BChang%26author%3DS.%2BH.%2BOh%26author%3DK.%2BY.%2BChung%26title%3DProbing%2Bthe%2BSodiation-Desodiation%2BReactions%2Bin%2BNano-sized%2BIron%2BFluoride%2BCathode%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2016.01.080&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c01652&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2016.01.080&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Sodiation-Desodiation Reactions in Nano-sized Iron Fluoride Cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ali, Ghulam; Lee, Ji-Hoon; Cho, Byung Won; Nam, Kyung-Wan; Ahn, Docheon; Chang, Wonyoung; Oh, Si Hyoung; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">191</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">307-316</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A hybrid nanocomposite of FeF3·0.5H2O and MWCNTs was synthesized as a high-performance cathode material for room-temp. Na-ion batteries.  The composite exhibits remarkably high capacity (197 mAh g-1) and stable cycle performance (148 mAh g-1 at the 100th cycle with a 0.05 C rate) accompanying with the unique nanostructure of the material and the incorporation of MWCNTs.  The addn. of MWCNTs not only increases the cond. of the active material but also plays a role to design a unique morphol. where nano-sized FeF3·0.5H2O particles are grown both inside and outside of the MWCNTs.  The Na diffusion coeff. of the composite material is detd. by galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique during dis/charging and the values are at 10-12-10-14 cm2 s-1 which are lesser than Li diffusion coeff. of FeF3 (10-10-10-12 cm2 s-1).  In situ x-ray diffraction coupled with ex situ high resoln. TEM is employed to study the phase transition behavior.  The results reveal both cryst. and amorphous phases upon Na insertion.  Also, ex situ NEXAFS spectroscopy (at the F K-edge and Fe L3-edge) is conducted at different potential steps to det. the change in oxidn. state and local structure.  NEXAFS spectra reveal the conversion reaction mechanism and reversibility of the material as evidenced by the de/formation of NaF during de/sodiation process.  The combined study of in situ XRD and NEXAFS will give valuable information on de/sodiation reactions in FeF3·0.5H2O.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1016/j.est.2018.04.026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hollow porous FeF<sub>3</sub> ·0.33H <sub>2</sub>O microspheres by AlPO<sub>4</sub> coating as a cathode material of Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>Journal of Energy Storage.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>18</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">103</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">111</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                10.1021/ja402061q</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D135%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D11425-11428%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DC.%2BLi%26author%3DC.%2BYin%26author%3DL.%2BGu%26author%3DR.%2BE.%2BDinnebier%26author%3DX.%2BMu%26author%3DP.%2BA.%2Bvan%2BAken%26author%3DJ.%2BMaier%26title%3DAn%2BFeF%25283%2529.0.5H2O%2Bpolytype%253A%2Ba%2Bmicroporous%2Bframework%2Bcompound%2Bwith%2Bintersecting%2Btunnels%2Bfor%2BLi%2Band%2BNa%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fja402061q&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c01652&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja402061q&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An FeF3·0.5H2O Polytype: A Microporous Framework Compound with Intersecting Tunnels for Li and Na Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Chilin; Yin, Congling; Gu, Lin; Dinnebier, Robert E.; Mu, Xiaoke; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To improve the energy/power d. of energy storage materials, numerous efforts have focused on the exploration of new structure prototypes, in particular metal-org. fameworks, Prussian blue analogs, open-framework oxides, and polyanion salts.  Here the authors report a novel pyrochlore phase that appears to be useful as a high-capacity cathode for Li and Na batteries.  It is an Fe fluoride polymorph characterized by an intersecting tunnel structure, providing the space for accommodation and transport of Li and Na ions.  It is prepd. using hydrolyzable ionic liqs., which serve as reaction educts and structure-directing agents not only as far as the chem. structure is concerned but also in terms of morphol. (shape, defect structure, electrode network structure).  A capacity &gt;220 mA h g-1 (for Li and Na storage) and a lifetime of ≥300 cycles (for Li storage) are demonstrated.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1021/ja902639w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamakawa, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Key, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grey, C. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Identifying the Local Structures Formed during Lithiation of the Conversion Material, Iron Fluoride, in a Li Ion Battery: A Solid-State NMR, X-ray Diffraction, and Pair Distribution Function Analysis Study</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem.                 10.1021/ja902639w</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D131%26publication_year%3D2009%26pages%3D10525-10536%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DN.%2BYamakawa%26author%3DM.%2BJiang%26author%3DB.%2BKey%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DIdentifying%2Bthe%2BLocal%2BStructures%2BFormed%2Bduring%2BLithiation%2Bof%2Bthe%2BConversion%2BMaterial%252C%2BIron%2BFluoride%252C%2Bin%2Ba%2BLi%2BIon%2BBattery%253A%2BA%2BSolid-State%2BNMR%252C%2BX-ray%2BDiffraction%252C%2Band%2BPair%2BDistribution%2BFunction%2BAnalysis%2BStudy%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fja902639w&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c01652&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja902639w&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Identifying the Local Structures Formed during Lithiation of the Conversion Material, Iron Fluoride, in a Li Ion Battery: A Solid-State NMR, X-ray Diffraction, and Pair Distribution Function Analysis Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamakawa, Naoko; Jiang, Meng; Key, Baris; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">131</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">30</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10525-10536</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural transformations that occur when FeF3 is cycled at room temp. in a Li cell were investigated using a combination of x-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) anal., and magic-angle-spinning NMR spectroscopy.  Two regions are seen on discharge.  The first occurs between Li = 0 and 1.0 and involves an insertion reaction.  This first region actually comprises two steps.  First, a two-phase reaction between Li = 0 and 0.5 occurs, and the Li0.5FeF3 phase that is formed gives rise to a Li NMR resonance due to Li+ ions near both Fe3+ and Fe2+ ions.  On the basis of the PDF data, the local structure of this phase is closer to the rutile structure than the original ReO3 structure.  Second, a single-phase intercalation reaction occurs between Li = 0.5 and 1.0, for which the Li NMR data indicate a progressive increase in the concn. of Fe2+ ions.  In the second region, the conversion reaction, superparamagnetic, nanosized (∼3 nm) Fe metal is formed, as indicated by the XRD and NMR data, along with some LiF and a third phase that is rich in Li and F.  The charge process involves the formation of a series of intercalation phases with increasing Fe oxidn. state, which, on the basis of the Li NMR and PDF data, have local structures that are similar to the intercalation phases seen during the first stage of the discharge process.  The solid-state NMR and XRD results for the rutile phase FeF2 are presented for comparison, and the data indicate that an insertion reaction also occurs, which is accompanied by the formation of LiF.  This is followed by the formation of Fe nanoparticles and LiF via a conversion reaction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1002/sia.740100203"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hawn, D.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Deconvolution as a correction for photoelectron inelastic energy losses in the core level XPS spectra of iron oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hawn, David D.; DeKoven, Benjamin M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Surface and Interface Analysis</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1987</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2-3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">63-74</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SIANDQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0142-2421</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Core level XPS spectra of polycryst. Fe metal and several oxides are cor. by deconvolution using the ratio method for energy loss effects and spectral broadening due to both an achromatic x-ray source and finite analyzer resoln.  These ideas are applied to the XPS spectra of Fe, FeO, Fe2O3, and Fe3O4 to obtain the intrinsic lineshape.  The backscattered electron energy loss spectra are effective in distinguishing Fe and its oxides.  For Fe metal and its oxides, the electron energy loss deconvolved spectra have a flat baseline and enhanced resoln.  This background removal method is more reliable than either linear or sigmoidal background approxns.  This allows for a detn. of the relative sensitivity factor for the Fe 2p and O 1s core levels.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187ff4cb7c075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-rich, Mn-based layered oxides Li2MnO3-LiMO2 (M=Ni, Co) have been considered as promising cathode candidates owing to their high capacity.  However, the resources shortage and high price of cobalt make it imperious to substitute cobalt with other high-abundance elements.  Here, we synthesized a low-cost, cobalt-free, Fe-substituted oxide material, Li(Li0.16Ni0.19Fe0.18Mn0.46)O2.  It exhibited a high reversible capacity of 169.2 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles and maintained an extraordinarily high discharge potential during cycling.  XPS and DFT calcns. revealed that super iron FeIV exists in the delithiated state, and oxygen participates in the redox reaction in addn. to the Ni2+/Ni4+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ redox couples.  The anionic oxidn. preferentially occurred on oxygen with a Li-O-Li configuration and with oxidized Fe and Ni coordination.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2016.08.080"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The FeF 3 ·0.33H 2 O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure as high-capacity cathode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                         rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure as high-capacity cathode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Shuangying; Wang, Xianyou; Jiang, Miaoling; Zhang, Rui; Shen, Yongqiang; Hu, Hai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The novel FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite with open mesoporous structure is successfully prepd. using a 1-pot chem. method and mech. ball milling route.  The structure and morphol. of the samples are examd. by XRD, SEM, high-resoln. TEM (HRTEM) and selected-area electron diffraction (SAED).  The as-synthesized FeF3·0.33H2O/C nanocomposite exhibits a particle size of ∼1-5 μm, open mesoporous structure with av. pore diams. of 16.5 nm.  Although the particles size is large up-to micron scale, the sample displays excellent electrochem. performance due to its open mesoporous structure, which is beneficial to the Li+ diffusion and electron transportation.  When FeF3·0.33H2O/C is used as cathode material of LIBs, it exhibits a specific capacity of 276.4 mA-h g-1 and excellent cyclic performance with a capacity of 193.5 mA-h g-1 after 50 cycles at 1 C for LIBs.  Also, it also exhibits a high reversible capacity of 102 mA-h g-1after 40 cycles at 1 C for SIBs.  Therefore, FeF3·0.33H2O/C will be a promising cathode material for high-performance LIBs and SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Advanced Energy Materials.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
